iii

ABSTRACT
Composite steel box girder bridges have become an effective alternative to more conventional plate girders and concrete girder bridges in North America in the past 25 years. At present, a number of innovations taking place in Canada and elsewhere have the potential of further enhancing the design, durability, constructability and, by extension, the economics of steel box- girder bridges. These innovations include reduction in the number of boxes. The main objective of this project is to detennine the optimum number of boxes for a given bridge required to reduce the cost of materials. This objective is achieved by designing and analyzing four different box-girder bridge for a four-lane bridge as well as three different box-girder bridge configurations for a three lane bridge, while meeting all design criteria, which are defined in the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code of 2000, CAN/ CSA-S6-00. The bridges studied here in are continuous over two-span. First, three, four, five and six-box girder cross sections were considered for four-lane bridges. While, three, four and five-box girder cross sections were considered for three-lane bridge. Both four lane and three lane bridge configurations were evaluated with respect to the most effective design for all straining action, including flexure, shear, deflection. Following the desing step, a detailed cost analysis was carried out for both four lane and three lane bridge configurations, to detennine the most cost-effective bridge configuration. Results show that, for four-lane bridges , the three box-girder bridge provided less material content than that obtained for each of the four, five, and six box-girder bridge configurations. Also, this study shows that, the three box-girder provided less material content than that for other bridge configurations considered for three lane bridge.

iv

It can be concluded that reduction in the number of boxes wherever possible in composite

concrete deck-over steel box-girder bridges would result in more economy in construction costs.

CHAPTER I

INTRODUCTION
1.1 General

The construction of box-girder bridges in interchanges of modem high highway systems has become increasingly popular for economic and aesthetic reasons. Box-girder cross sections may take the form of single cell (one box), multi spine (separate boxes), or multi cell with a common bottom flange as shown in Figure 1.1 . The current design specifications in North America [the AASHTO stand art specification of Highway Bridges of 1996 (3) , the AASHTO LRFD Bridge Design Specification of 2000 (1) and the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code of 2000 (59)] recommend few analytical methods for the analysis of certain types of straight box-girder bridges as well as load-distribution factors for longitudinal moment and shear. Box-girder bridges have the following advantages over typical I-beam bridge systems: (i) low material content, relatively light weight, (ii) high bending rigidity, (iii) composite concrete deck-steel cellular bridges provide significantly high torsional rigidity when compared with open-section bridges, (iv) high stability during the erection process, and (v) high vertical clearance due to the favourable span-to-depth ratio.

1.2 The Problem

All of the box-girder bridge configurations shown in Figure 1.1 , the multiple box-girder cross-section has been used extensively in North American highways, because of the simple analysis availability of simple analysis of such bridge cross-section configuration. Figure 1.2 shows typical twin-box section bridge commonly used in United States. It consists of reinforced concrete deck supported over steel boxes. Each steel box consists of two top flanges and a bottom flange connected to the top flanges by webs. A full interaction between top flanges and the reinforced concrete deck slab is ensured by shear connectors. Solid steel end diaphragms are used at the support lines. The current design practices in North America specifies simplified design method for composite concrete-steel multiple box-girder bridges in the form of expressions for bending stress and shear distribution factors. These expressions can predict the bending moment and vertical shear to be carried by each box.

3

However it does not provide indications on the economy of box-girder design as a result of a reduction in the number of boxes.

1.3 Objectives and Scope The objectives of this study is to: (i) Analyze and efficiently design different cross-section configurations of fourlane and three-lane bridge superstructure usin the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code, CAN/ CSA-S6-00. (ii) Determine the material content of each bridge to decide on the most economical bridge configuration.

The performance of three-box, four-box, five-box, and six-box girder cross section for a four-lane bridge and three-box, four-box and five-box girder cross section for a three-lane bridge is studied using the Canadian Highway Bridge design Code (59). These seven different bridge configurations are continuous over two spans. Both spans are 48.00 m each. The span-to-depth ratios of the bridges are kept constant as 30. For four-lane bridges, the width comprises a 15.00 m-wide deck supporting four traffic lanes and flanked by 2.00 mwide sidewalks. While, for the three-lane bridges, the 14.15 m bridge width comprises a 11.25 m-wide deck supporting three traffic lanes and flanked by 1.00 m-wide sidewalks.

4

1.4 Contents and Arrangements of the Degree Project
The literature review is presented in Chapter II. Chapter III explains the design procedure, and the method, which is used in the analysis, for four-lane, three-box girder bridge case. Chapter IV gives the results of moment, shear resistance of webs, control of permanent deflections and fatigue stress range resistance for four-lane and three lane bridge configurations considered in this study. It also presents a summary and comparison of material content of the bridges. Chapter V presents the conclusion of the research work. A CD is attached for six appendices. These appendices show the detailed design procedure for each bridge configuration, considered in this study other than the four-lane, three-box bridge type, which is covered in chapter III.

5

CHAPTER II

LITERATURE REVIEW
2.1 General
Composite concrete deck-over steel box-girder bridges are considered more efficient than steel I-girder bridges. They are characterized by high torsion resistance when compared to 1girders, thus enhancing the transverse live load distribution. A significant incidental advantage is the significant decrease in transverse moment in the deck slab due to better load sharing between closed boxes and less differential deflections between adjacent girders. This chapters presents a summary of the literature review pertained to (i) the load distribution and Codes of Practice for box-girder bridge design, and (ii) the economical steel box-girder bridges.

2.2 Load Distribution and Codes of Practice for Box Girder Bridges
Composite girder bridges may take the form of I-girder, multi-cells, or multiple-spines. Bridges of concrete deck over steel boxes are more efficient and economical than those of composite steel-concrete I-girders since they exhibit better transverse load distribution due to their superior torsional stiffness. Further economies can be achieved by using a multiple box bridge section other than a cellular bridge section. The former weighs less than the latter, generally. The following paragraphs provide a summary of the research work done on load distribution in such bridge types.

In 1985, Yoo and Littrell (30) studied the effect of cross-bracing on the warping and bending stresses of curved I-girders using a full three-dimensional finite-element modelling. In 1986,

6

Brockenbrough (7) used the finite-element modelling to derive load distribution factors, including the warping effects, of curved composite I-girder bridges as a function of the span length, radius of curvature, girder spacing, and cross-bracing spacing. In 1967, and 1968, 10hanston and Mattock (18), and Fountain and Mattock (13) used a computer program for the analysis of folded plate structures to study the lateral distribution of load in simple span composite multiple-spine box girder bridges without transverse diaphragms. To verify their analysis and computer program, a one-quarter scale model of a two-lane, 80 ft span bridge supported by three box girders, and one-fifth scale model of a two-lane, 100 ft span bridge supported by two box girders were built and tested under concentric and eccentric AASHTO truck loadings. The results were used to develop an expression for the live load bending moment distribution factor for each box girder as a function of the roadway width, and number of boxes. Their findings formed the basis for the lateral distribution of loads for bending moment currently used by AASHTO (3) and by the Ontario Highway Bridge Design Code in 1983 (25) for the multi-spine box girder bridges. The drawback of this expression is that the beneficial effect of the cross-bracing inside and between the boxes was not taken into account. Moreover, it limits its application to bridges with a number of spines equal to the number of lanes. The AASHTO Load Resistance Factor Design (1) provides another expression for load distribution for the ultimate limit state to obtain the live load bending moment and shear force in each box of the multiple-spine box girder bridge cross-section.

In 1985, and 1992, Bakht and Jaeger (4,5) presented a particular case of multiple-spine bridges

having at least three spines, zero transverse bending stiffuess, with the load transfer between the various spines through transverse shear. Based on these simplifications, they proposed load

7

distribution factors for bending moment and shear. These formed the basis for live load distribution used by the third edition of the OHBDC (26) for multiple-spine bridges. In 1994, Normandin and Massicotte (22) presented the results of a refined finite-element analysis to determine the distribution patterns in multiple-spine box girder bridges with different characteristics and geometry. Several parameters were considered in the analysis: the presence of the internal diaphragms, the use of external bracing, and the type of live load. The study proved that internal diaphragms, inside boxes, are essential components in a box girder bridge since they contribute largely to the reduction of the cross-sectional distortion. Moreover, external bracings between boxes do not significantly influence the distribution characteristics of bending moments and shear, since a fully loaded bridge usually governs the design. Results also indicate that, in some cases, both the AASHTO (3) and OHBDC (26) distribution factors underestimate the live load effects by a significant margin.

In 1978, Heins (15) extended the applicability of the straight mUltiple-spine box girder moment

distribution equation provided by Fountain and Mattock (l3) to horizontally curved composite multiple-spine box girder bridges. A modification factor to the straight girder moment distribution was proposed as a function of the radius of curvature provided that cross-bracings are used inside the boxes. In 1980, Mukherjee and Trikha (21), using the finite-strip method, developed a set of design coefficients for twin cell curved box girder reinforced concrete bridges as an aid to practical design of such bridges. These coefficients were for moment, shear, transverse moment, and vertical deflection under the webs. These coefficients were limited only to concrete bridges of two-lane, span length between 20 and 40 m, and radius of curvature between 45 and 150 m.

8

The AASHTO Guide Specification for Horizontally Curved Highway Bridges (2) pertained to both curved composite concrete deck-steel I-girder bridges and multiple-spine box girder bridges. The specifications for load distribution for both curved I-girders and multiple-spine box girders were based on a design-oriented research work done by Heins and Jin in 1984 (16) on live load distribution of single and continuous curved composite I-girder bridges. They utilized a space frame modelling to incorporate the interaction of diaphragms (cross-bracing in the radial direction) and bottom lateral bracing in some bays or in all bays. Appropriate design equations were presented for use in conjunction with a plane grid solution. For both curved I-girders and curved multiple-spine box girders, the AASHTO Guide Specification for Horizontally Curved Highway Bridges of 1993 (2) specifies that the moments and shears required to proportion the individual members shall be based on a rational analysis, other than a plane grid, of the entire structure which takes into account the complete distribution of loads to the various members. Otherwise, the code modifies the resulting maximum live load stresses, using the Heins-Jin design equation, considering additional warping stresses besides the normal bending stresses obtained from plane grid analysis.

In 1981, Davis and Bon (11) presented a correction factor for curvature for load distribution in concrete and prestressed concrete multi-cell box girder bridges. For all girders except the

exterior girder farthest from the centre of curvature, this factor is a ratio of the distance from the centre of curvature to the assigned girder to the centre-line radius of curvature of the bridge. For the outermost girder (farthest from the centre of curvature), they proposed straight bridge load distribution without any modifications. The drawback of this method is that the beneficial effect of the transverse diaphragms was not taken into consideration. In 1988, Nutt et al. (23) proposed

9

a set of equations for moment distribution in straight, reinforced and prestressed concrete, multicell box girder bridges as a function of the number of lanes, cell width, span length, and number of cells. In 1989, Ho et al. (17) used the finite-strip method to analyse straight simply-supported, two-cell box girder and rectangular voided slab bridges without intermediate diaphragms. Empirical expressions were deduced for the ratio of the maximum longitudinal bending moment to the equivalent beam moment. This study was limited to a straight two-cell bridge section made of either steel or concrete. Moreover, the span lengths of the bridges in this study were up to 40 m in case of two-lane, 50 m in case of three-lane, and 67 m in case of four-lane. In 1995, using the finite-strip method, Cheung and Foo (9) proposed expressions for the ratio moment distribution factor of the curved multiple-spine box girder bridge to that of the straight one as a function of span length, number of lanes, box spacing, and radius of curvature. The disadvantage of this study is that it did not take into account the beneficial effect of diaphragms inside the boxes or cross-bracings between boxes. Further, the effect of the number of boxes and dead load distribution were not included.

The AASHTO code of 1996 (3) specified load distribution factors for bending moment in straight reinforced concrete box girder bridges, namely: S/8 for one-lane traffic and SI7 for two or more traffic lanes, where S is the cell width in feet. The specified load distribution factors were based on suggestions made by California design engineers in 1959 (10). However, these distribution factors do not give the designer any indication on the behaviour of the bridge and the parameters influencing the response of the structure other than the cell width. In 1991, Zokaie et al. (28, 31) proposed other moment and shear distribution factors for reinforced and prestressed concrete multi-cell bridges. Their findings form the load distribution factors for

10

moment and shear currently used by the AASHTO LRFD code (1) for straight concrete multicell bridges. In 1996, Brighton et al. (6) described a study to determine a live load distribution factor for a new type of precast concrete double cell box girders that was proposed for a prefabricated bridge system for rapid construction of short-span bridges. In 1994 and 1996, Dean (12) and Fu et al. (14) studied torsion on multi cellular members. In 1998, using the finiteelement method, Sennah (27) proposed moment, shear, and deflection distribution factors, as well as maximum force in bracing, for curved composite concrete deck-steel cellular bridges.

Some of the aforementioned investigations on box girder bridges were confined to reinforced and prestressed concrete construction, and did not include composite concrete deck-steel construction. Others dealt with steel box girders but were limited to particular cases, or in their range of applicability of the research findings. Therefore, research work on moment and shear distribution of straight and curved composite concrete deck-steel multiple-spine bridges is required. The Canadian codes of practice for highway bridge design [OHBDC, 1992 (26); CAN/CSA-S6-88 (8); CHBDC, 2000 (24)] have no specifications for the design of horizontally curved bridges including those of multi-box cross-sections, while the American code of practice for horizontally curved highway bridges [AASHTO Guide Specification, 1993 (2)] provides load distribution factors for curved composite bridges other than those of multi-box crosssection. Because of its superior torsional-to-bending stifihess ratio when compared to other types of bridge cross-sections, the multiple-spine bridge cross-section is required, specially, in sharply curved alignments to allow better load distribution characteristics. The American Association of State Highway and Transportation Officials code of 1996 (3) states the curved bridge may be designed as a straight bridge if the central angle ~ 12°. On the other hand, the

11

Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code of 1997 (24) states that for horizontally curved bridges, the effect of curvature may be neglected in structural design calculations as long as .two conditions are met: L2/bR ratio is less than 1.0, where L is the curved span length, b is half the bridge width, and R is the radius of curvature; or, when R > 90 m. These limitations are questionable in the case of moment and shear in curved composite multiple-spine bridges.

2.3 Economical Steel Box Girder Bridges

There have been a few bridge projects recently for which the designers have reduced the number of boxes to support a given cross section (19). The result has been a corresponding increase in the number of design lanes per box. Some owners and agencies have encouraged this trend through the technical review and approvals process and through inclusion of provisions in current codes that allow designers to move outside the limitations of the original criteria published in 1965, provided that they can demonstrate an appropriate methodology for analysis and design. Significant economic advantage can be achieved by reducing the number of boxes required to support a given cross section.

A significant incidental benefit is the reduced transverse bending in the deck due to better load sharing between boxes and corresponding reductions in differential deflections. Canadian designers have typically designed bridges for one design lane per box (20). Redundancy becomes an issue in multispine bridges where the number of spines is reduced. As the number of girders is decreased, the ratio of dead load to live load increases. This in tum reduces the stress ranges for fatigue and the potential for fatigue problems.

12

CHAPTER III

DESIGN PROCEDURE
3.1 General

The main objective of this research work presented here is to design and optimize four different box-girder bridge configurations for four-lane bridge and three different bridge configurations for three-lane bridge, to meet the design criteria, in accordance with the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (24). All seven bridges are class A, undivided urban roadways. The bridges are continuous over two spans, with span length of 48. m each. Each bridge cross-section has equally spaced trapezoidal steel box girders with a depth of 1.6 m. Four-lane bridges have a 19.9 m bridge width, comprising a 15. m-wide deck supporting 4 traffic lanes and flanking 2. m-wide sidewalks. While three-lane bridge have a bridge 14.15 m bridge width comprising a 11.25 m -wide deck, supporting three traffic lanes and flanking 1. m-wide sidewalks. The simplified method of analysis specified by the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code, CHBDC, (24) is used for lateral live load distribution. While, the Empirical Design method, specified by the CHBDC is used to design the concrete deck slabs for all bridges. All calculations were carried out on spread sheets in the Microsoft Excel Program. SAP 2000 structural analysis software (29) was used to analyze the idealized twospan girder. The following sections presents the analysis and design procedure of the fourlane, three box-girder bridge with brief references to other bridge configurations considered in this study. Complete design procedure and results for bridge configurations other than the four-lane, three-box girder bridge can be found in the CD enclosed with the degree project.

3.2 Box Girder Bridges The following four sections of Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code are used to analyze and design box girder bridges considered in this study.

Section 3 (Loads): It contains information about Limit states criteria, load factors and load
combinations, dead loads, and live loads.

Section 5 (Methods of Analysis) : It includes information about deflection, structural
response, analysis of decks, longitudinal bending moments, longitudinal shear for fatigue limit state, and superstructure vibrations.

Section 8 (Concrete Structures) : It includes information about cracking control,
deformation control, reinforcing bars, fatigue limit state, transverse reinforcement, non-

composite and composite action, and flange and web thickness.

Section 10 (Steel Structures) : It includes information about design theory, compression and
tension members, composite box girders, moment resistance, compression flanges stiffened longitudinally and transversely, and structural fatigue.

3.3 Design Procedure 3.3.1 Design Code: Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code of 2000, CAN/CSA-S6-00, is currently the National Bridge Code in all Canadian provinces and it is considered as the basis for analysis and design of bridges in this research work.

3.3.2 Roadway: Bridges considered in this study are Class A, four- and three-lane undivided urban roadway.

16

3.3.3. Design Approach Longitudinal moments and shear forces are calculated using the "simplified method of analysis" for both dead and live loads, in accordance with Clauses 5.6.1 and 5.7.1 of the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (24). The "empirical method of analysis" is used for the deck slab in accordance with Clauses 8.18.4 and 8.15.5.

3.4 Material Properties 3.4.1 Steel: The structural steel used in bridge design is 040.21 grade 350A and 350AT (Atmospheric resistant, notch-tough) with modulus of elasticity, Es. 200,000 Mpa, Shear Modulus, Os 77,000 Mpa. The minimum specified yield stress, Fy , is taken as 350 Mpa for structural steel , while the yield stress is taken as fy = 400 Mpa for the reinforcing bars. The main density of steel is taken as 7800 kg I m3 ; with material density of 77 kN I m 3 .

3.4.2 Concrete: The 28-day concrete compressive strength, ( , is taken as 30 MPa , with modulus of elasticity, Ee, of 24,800 MPa according to Clause 8.4.1.7 of the CHBDC. The mass density of concrete, ,,{, is taken as 2400 kg I m3 , with material density of23.5 kNI m3 ·

3.4.3 Design Lanes: For four-lane, three-box section shown in Figure 3.1, the deck width, We, is taken as 15. m, with number of design lanes ,n, of 4 . The design lane width is taken as follows based on Clause 3.8.2: We = We I n = 15 14 = 3.75 m.

17

Modification factor for multi-lane loading, R L , is taken as 0.7 for number of lanes of four according to Clause 3.8.4.2.

3.4.4 Dynamic Load Allowance:

For 48 m span bridge, truck load of3 axles or more is used. So the dynamic load allowance, DLA, is taken 0.25 as specified in Clause 3.8.4.5.

3.4.5 Vibrations and Serviceability:

The bridge is designed for occasional pedestrian use.

3.5 Cross-Section Design:

For three-lane bridge section, a 1600 mm web depth is selected, resulting in a span-to-depth ratio of 30. The roadway deck features a 225 mm concrete slab topped by a 90 mm asphalt wearing surface.

3.5.1 Requirements of the "Simplified Method of Analysis" for Live Load
Clause 5.7.1.1 of the CHBDC (24) specifies few limitations for the use of the simplified design method of analysis as follows: a) the bridge width is constant b) support conditions corresponds to line supports c) skew limits are satisfied (the bridge is unskewed) d) horizontal curvature limits are satisfied (The bridge is straight) e) the length of the slab taper near free edges does not exceed 2.5 m

18

f) there are at least 3 equally spaced longitudinal girders of equal flexural rigidity g) slab overhang is less than 60% the spacing between two outennost webs. h) the provisions A5.1(a) ofCHBDC (24) apply as follows,

When the simplified methods given in Clauses 5.7.1.2 through 5.7.1.5 ofCHBDC (24) are to be for a multi span bridge, the effect of continuity F shall be taken as; for positive moments in an exterior span, 80% of the distance between the external support and the internal support; for positive moments in an interior span, 60% of the distance between the internal supports; For negative moments in the region of an internal support, 25% of the sum of the spans on either side of the support.

i) each spine (or box) has only 2 webs, and the conditions of Clause 10.12.5.1 of CHBDC (24) apply. This clause covers flange cover restrictions, lateral support, flange to web connections, transverse stiffener restrictions.

3.5.2 Requirements of the "Empirical Design Method" of Deck Slabs Clause 8.18.4.1 of the CHBDC (24) specifies limitations for the use the simplified design method for deck slabs as follows: a) The deck slab is composite with the supporting, parallel girders b) Maximum girder web spacing is less than 18 times the slab thickness . c) The maximum girder web spacing is less than 4000 mm d) The requirements of section 10 ofCHBDC (24), which defines the material properties of steel bars, longitudinal regarding longitudinal reinforcement apply, and

19

r
e) The "empirical design method" requires a maximum intermediate cross frame spacing of8 m as stated in Clause 8.18.5. For limitation "b" and "c" , maximum spacing of3550 mm as shown in Figure 3.6 is less than 18 times slab thickness of 225 mm (18 x 225 = 4050 mm) and less than 4000 mm.

3.5.3 Requirements for Multiple Box Girders To meet the requirements of the definition of multiple box-girder configuration stated in Clause 10.12.7.1, the following limitation are set: a) The centre-to centre flange spacing is constant. b) Centre-to-centre flange spacing: The ratio of flange spacing between adjacent boxes to flange spacing between boxes more than 0.8 but less than 1.20. In case of four-lane, three-box-girder bridge shown in Figure 3.6 , this criteria is met. flange spacing between adjacent boxes = 3550 3100 flange spacing within boxes

= 1.15

c) Cantilever slab overhang should be less than 60% of spacing between top flanges of adjacent boxes. In case of four-lane, three-box-girder bridge shown in Figure 3.6, the cantilever slab overhang is 1750 mm which is less than 60% of the assigned spacing (0.6 x 3550 = 2130 mm).

3.5.4 Inflexion Points For the purpose of calculating lengths, L, effective slab widths, and live load amplification factors, inflexion points of two-span continuous beam subjected to dead load are given as follows:

3.7.2 Bottom Flange The effective bottom flange width of box girders is calculated according to Clause 10.12 .2, which states that the effective width of bottom flange plates in tension shall be taken as not more than one-fifth of the distance between points of contraflexure. Under dead load for continuous structures. using lengths, which is given as 0.8 L\ as indicated in Fig. A5.l(a) ofCHBDC (24) L= 0.8 x 48,000 = 38,400 mm

Bottom Flange width = 2300 mm < 38,400 / 5 =7680 mm The full range is effective.

3.8 Slab Reinforcement
Since the cross-frame spacing does not exceed 8 m, the "empirical design method" (Clause 8.18.4) is used for the slab reinforcement.

3.8.1 Minimum Concrete Cover
Table 8.11.2.2 of the CHBDC (24) provides information about the minimum concrete cover. For an environmental exposure including de-icing chemicals, cast-in place concrete: Rebar diameter, 15M: 16.0 mm Top rebars, minimum cover = 70 mm Top longitudinal rebar depth = 70 + 16 + 16 / 2 = 94 mm Minimum cover for bottom rebars (soffit, slab thickness < 300 mm) = 50 mm Bottom transverse rebar depth (for calculating effective depth of concrete, d, lower reinforcing assembly, Clause 8. 18.4.2.(a) =225-50-16/2= 167mm.

3.8.2 Empirical Slab Design Method Requirements
Slab thickness: 225 mm > 175 mm, Clause 8.18.2 Reinforcement ratio, p ~ 0.003, Clause 8.18.4.2(a) Rebar spacing:::; 300 mm, Clause 8.18.4.2 (e)

3.8.5 Transverse Slab Moments in the Cantilever Overhang Since the empirical design method is used for the transverse reinforcement, only the transverse moments due to loads on the overhang need be checked (Clause 8.18.1). Moments due to wheel loads on sidewalk are calculated using CHBDC Clauses 3.8.4.4 and 5.7.1.6.1. Design assumptions for this example are based on the 225 mm structural slab only, conservatively ignoring the stiffness contributions of the sidewalk and barrier.

Slab overhang: Sc = 1.30 m, C = 1.75 - 0.75 = 1.0 m Slab thickness at the cantilever root, haunch and assuming 60 mm steel flange: t2 = 225 + (75-60 ) = 240 mm , tl = 225 mm , rt = tl / t2 = 225 /240 = 0.9375 Assuming an unstiffened edge, rt = 0.938 and Sc = 1.30 m . Tandem axles 2 + 3 (P = 62.5 kN) do not govern in this case.

3.9 Section Properties
Four different sets of section properties are calculated, depending on the construction stage and type of loading: 1. 2. 3. Before composite action bare steel girder only After composite action, 1 x n: live loads (Sn ) After composite action, 3 x n : dead loads applied after composite action, to account for creep and shrinkage an accordance with Clause 10.11.3 (S3n) 4. After composite action, negative moment regions: steel girder and the reinforcing bars only (S')

3.9.1 Steel- Concrete Modular Ratio
Short-term loads: n = Es / Ec = 200,000 / 24,800 = 8.06 Long-term loads (including creep and shrinkage): 3n = 3 x 8.06 = 24.2

3.9.2 Stiffness Analysis
Negative bending moments at the supports are computed assuming full structural continuity, that is, using uncracked composite sections. Therefore, the analysis is based on fully composite sections using a modular ratio of 1 x n in both positive and negative moment regions. Figures 3.6 and 3.7 show dimensions and thicknesses for four-lane, three-box girder bridge considered herein.

3.11 Simplified Method of Analysis - Live Load Amplification Factors
Live load amplification factors (Fm and Fv) enable the calculation of longitudinal moments and shear forces using a simple, one-dimensional, line element mode, provided the requirements the "Simplified Method of Analysis" given in Clause 5.7.1. are satisfied.

51

3.11.3 Summary of Live Load Amplification Factors

Based on the procedure to calculate load distribution factors in sub-sections 3.11.1 and 3.11.2, negative moment, positive moment and shear force distribution factors are calculated and than listed in Table 3.21. These values are calculated for ultimate limit states (ULS), serviceability limit state (SLS) and Fatigue Limit State (FLS).

3.11.4 Moments After Composite Action, ULS

CHBDC (24) specifies live loads on bridges in the form of truck loading and pedestrian loads. Truck loading consists of either CL-625 truck load as shown in Figure 3.8-a or lane loading as shown in Figure 3.8-b. Pedestrian load, shown in Figure 3.8-c, consists of uniform load over the designated area for pedestrians. For two-span beam of 48.00 m length for each span, dead load ,before composite action (steel + slab), moments are calculated ,using SAP 2000 software, at the 21 span reference points. Similar procedure is done for dead load after composite action but before applying live load (barrier + wearing surface). Then, influence lines of bending moment due to truck loading, lane loading and pedestrian load are calculated. Then these values are combined by applying the proper load factors and load combination factors as specified by the CHBDC (24). These analysis steps are recorded in details in Table 3.22 for ultimate limit state design (ULS). Figure 3.3 summarizes the factored moment envelopes for all loading cases considered for ULS after composite action is in place.

52

3.11.5 Moments After Composite Action, SLS/FLS

The previous step for ULS in sub-section 3.11.4 is repeated but for serviceability limit state and fatigue limit state (SLSI FLS). The main difference is focused on the load factor, the load distribution factor, Fm, and the load combination. Table 3.23 summarize bending moments at span reference points for load combinations required for SLS and FLS.

3.11.6 Shear Forces After Composite Action, ULS

Shear forces are calculated at span reference points for unfactored dead load, truck loading, lane loading, and pedestrian loading. Then load factors and load combination factors are applied to obtain the maximum factored shear forces for ULS. Table 3.24 summarizes the results of each step, while Figure 3.4 shows the factored shear force envelope for the different load combinations.

3.12.4 Shear Resistance

The shear resistance is calculated in accordance with Clauses 10.10.5.1 and 10.12.3. In the following example, the shear resistance of the trapezoidal box girder, V r, is taken equal to the sum of the resistances of each web, multiplied by cose , where e is the angle of inclination of the web plate with respect to vertical. The stiffened box girder has been divided longitudinally into different zones as shown in Figure 3.6, in addition to the anchor panel zone located at the abutments, to determine shear resistance. Each zone has a different web stiffener spacing. The resisting shear stress of a stiffened web panel consists of two components, Fs = Fer + Ft, where Fer is the shear buckling stress, and Ft is the tension field component.

Web inclination angle: e = 14.0° , cose = 0.970 Factored shear force applied on the girder:

3.14 Shear Resistance of Anchor Panels 3.14.1 Anchor Panel Zone, Near Abutments It is usually impractical to provide effective means of anchoring the tension field

in an end panel. Therefore, the end panels are proportioned to serve as anchor panels such that tension fields can be developed in the interior panels. The shear resistance of end panels is calculated by setting Ft = 0 (Clause 10.10.5.1).

3.15 Control of Cracking, SLS The requirements for control of cracking of the concrete slab are given in CHBDC Clause 8.12. However, since the slab reinforcement is designed in accordance with the empirical design method, these requirements are waived (Clause 8.18.1).

3.16 Superstructure Vibrations, SLS The maximum deflection due to a single truck, including dynamic allowance, should not exceed the limit given in CHBDC Figure 3.4.4. The deflection limit depends on the fundamental frequency of the bridge and the anticipated pedestrian use. Because the two spans are equal, a simplified method will be used to estimate the fundamental frequency.

3.17 Fatigue Fatigue stress ranges are based on FLS moments computed using the live load of a single truck, Clause 5.7.1.2.2 of CHBDC (24). Load induced .fatigue may be disregarded at locations where the compressive stress due to permanent loads is at least twice the maximum tensile stress due to the live loads, Clause 10.17.2.1 of CHBDC (24). The following design requirement must be satisfied.

0.52 fsr < Fsr , Clause 10.17.2.2 ofCHBDC (24).

where fsr is the calculated stress range due to the passage of a CL-W truck, while Fsr is the fatigue stress range resistance of a member or a detail, Clause 10.17.2.3 of CHBDC (24).

3.17.1 Detail Categories

Top flange: Category C (stud-type shear connectors), Clause 10.17.2.4

Example 6, Figure 10.17.2.4 of CHBDC (24).

Reinforcing Bars
Fatigue requirements do not apply to transverse reinforcing bars designed according to the Empirical Design Method (8.18.4), but fatigue must be considered for longitudinal bars that are part of composite girders (CHBDC Commentary 8.5.3.1). Maximum fatigue stress range for rebars, Clause 8.5.3.1 of CHBDC (24) For Straight bars: 125 MPa At anchorages, connections, bends, and welded splices: 65 MPa

3.17.3 Fatigue Stress Range, Bottom Flange, Span Reference Point 5 Factored dead load moment before composite action (FLS, a.D = 1.0): Mfd = 6787 kN.m Factored dead load moment after composite action (FLS, a.D = 1.0): Mfsd = 4419 kN.m S = 84.7 S3n = 99.1 Total dead load stress = Mfd / S + Mfsd / S3n = 6787/84.7 + 4419 / 99.1 = 80.13

+ 44.59 = 124.72 MPa

Since the permanent stress (124 MPa) is tensile, fatigue requirements apply. Figure 3.10 Effective Slab Width, for the Four-Lane, Three-Box Girder Bridge

81

CHAPTER IV

RESULTS
4.1. Results Summary for Four-Lane Bridge Design

This section presents the results for four lane bridge configurations.
4.1.1 Three-Box Girder Bridge Design Results

Figure 3.6 shows the cross-section dimensions of the four-lane, three-box girder bridge and longitudinal and transverse reinforcements in the rectangular concrete block within the effective slab width. Figure 3.7 shows that the bridge is divided into 20 segments, ten in each span. This implies 21 span reference points starting from the left end to the right end of the bridge. It should be noted 11 is located at the pier. Figure 3.8 presents CHBDC (24) live loads as truck load, lane load and pedestrian load. Figure 3.9 represents some parameters and loading condition to calculate cantilever moments. Figure 3.10 shows the total effective slab width for one box-girder for four-lane, three-box girder bridge.

Table 4.1 shows the thicknesses and lengths of three-box girder bridge elements, such as slab, top flange, webs, bottom flange at each segment of the three-box girder bridge. Table 4.2 shows the calculation of moment resistance before composite action. It presents factored moment, Mr , and moment resistance, before composite action, Mr , and the moment performance of the four-lane, three-box girder bridge. Table 4.3 presents resistance of composite sections for four-lane, three-box girder bridge. It checks the plastic neutral axis and calculates the depth of compressive stress block. Table 4.4 presents shear resistance for four-lane, three-box girder bridge. It presents the factored shear and factored moments at "each section, it shows the calculation ofFer and Ft for specific web stiffener distances, shear

82

resistance,Vr , moment resistance, Mr, and combined shear and moment situation. Table 4.5 shows control of permanent deflections, SLS for four lane, three box girder bridge using Clause 10.11.4 of CHBDC (24) . It presents Md (SLS), Msd (SLS), ML (SLS), Mfd (ULS), Mfsd(ULS), MfL(ULS) and sectional properties of the four-lane, three-box girder bridge at each section. It also shows the calculation of total factored tension at top and bottom flanges for composite sections at the positive and negative moment regions. Table 4.6 presents fatigue stress range resistance, SLS for four lane, three box girder bridge. It shows sectional modulus of the three-box girder bridge at each section for composite and non-composite cases. It also presents Mfd (FLS), Mfsd (FLS),and MiL (FLS). It shows the calculation of fatigue stress range resistance as well.

4.1.2 Four-Box Girder Bridge Design Results

Figure 4.1 shows the cross-section dimensions of the four-lane, four-box girder bridge and longitudinal and transverse reinforcements in the rectangular concrete block within the effective slab width. Figure 4.2 shows that the bridge is divided into 20 segments, lOin each span. This implies 21 span reference points starting from the left end to the right end of the bridge. It should be noted 11 is located at the pier. Figure 4.3 shows the total effective slab width for one box-girder for four-lane, four-box girder bridge.

Table 4.7 shows the thicknesses and lengths of four-box girder bridge elements, such as slab, top flange, webs, bottom flange at each segment of the four-box girder bridge. Table 4.8 shows the calculation of moment resistance before composite action. It presents factored moment, Mf , and moment resistance, before composite action, Mr , and the moment performance of the four-lane, four-box girder bridge before composite action. Table 4.9 presents resistance of composite sections ,for positive moment, for four-lane, four-box girder bridge. It checks the plastic neutral axis and calculates the depth of compressive stress block. Table 4.10 presents shear resistance for four-lane, four-box girder bridge. It presents the factored shear and factored moments at each section, it shows the calculation ofFer and Ft for specific web stiffener distances, shear resistance,V r, moment resistance, Mr, and combined shear and moment case. Table 4.11 shows control of permanent deflections, SLS for four lane, four- box girder bridge using Clause 10.11.4 of CHBDC (24). It presents Md (SLS), Msd (SLS), ML (SLS), Mfd (ULS), Mfsd(ULS), MfL(ULS) and sectional properties of the fourlane, four-box girder bridge at each section. It also shows the calculation of total factored . tension at top and bottom flanges for composite sections at the positive and negative

4.1.3 Five-Box Girder Bridge Design Results Summary

Figure 4.4 shows the cross-section dimensions of the four-lane, five-box girder bridge and longitudinal and transverse reinforcements in the rectangular concrete block within the effective slab width. Figure 4.5 shows that the bridge is divided into 20 segments, lOin each span. This implies 21 span reference points starting from the left end to the right end of the bridge. It should be noted 11 is located at the pier. Figure 4.6 shows the total effective slab width for one box-girder for four-lane, five-box girder bridge.

Table 4.13 shows the thicknesses and lengths of five-box girder bridge elements, such as slab, top flange, webs, bottom flange at each segment of the five-box girder bridge. Table 4.14 shows the calculation of moment resistance before composite action. It presents factored moment, Mr , and moment resistance, before composite action, Mr , and the moment performance of the four-lane, five-box girder bridge before composite action. Table 4.15 presents resistance of composite sections ,for positive moment, for four-lane, five-box girder bridge. It checks the plastic neutral axis and calculates the depth of compressive stress block. Table 4.16 presents shear resistance for four-lane, five-box girder bridge. It presents the factored shear and factored moments at each section, it shows the calculation ofFer and Ft for specific web stiffener distances, shear resistance,V r, moment resistance, Mr, and combined shear and moment case. Table 4.17 shows control of permanent deflections, SLS for four lane, five- box girder bridge using Clause 10.11.4 of CHBDC (24) . It presents Md (SLS), Msd (SLS), ML (SLS), M rd (ULS), Mrsd(ULS), MtL(ULS) and sectional properties of the four-lane, five-box girder bridge at each section. It also shows the calculation of total factored tension at top and bottom flanges for composite sections at the positive and negative moment

4.1.4 Six box girder bridge design results summary Figure 4.7 shows the cross-section dimensions of the four-lane, six-box girder bridge and longitudinal and transverse reinforcements in the rectangular concrete block within the effective slab width. Figure 4.8 shows that the bridge is divided into 20 segments, lOin each span. This implies 21 span reference points starting from the left end to the right end of the bridge. It should be noted 11 is located at the pier. Figure 4.9 shows the total effective slab width for one box-girder for four-lane, six-box girder bridge.

Table 4.19 shows the thicknesses and lengths of six-box girder bridge elements, such as slab, top flange, webs, bottom flange at each segment of the six-box girder bridge. Table 4.20 shows the calculation of moment resistance before composite action. It presents factored moment, Mr , and moment resistance, before composite action, Mr , and the moment performance of the four-lane, six-box girder bridge before composite action. Table 4.21 presents resistance of composite sections ,for positive moment, for four-lane, six-box girder bridge. It checks the plastic neutral axis and calculates the depth of compressive stress block. Table 4.22 presents shear resistance for four-lane, six-box girder bridge. It presents the factored shear and factored moments at each section, it shows the calculation ofFer and Ft for specific web stiffener distances, shear resistance,V r, moment resistance, M r, and combined shear and moment case. Table 4.23 shows control of permanent deflections, SLS for four lane, six-box girder bridge using Clause 10.11.4 of CHBDC (24) . It presents Md (SLS), Msd
(SLS), ML (SLS), Mfd (ULS), Mfsd(ULS), MfL(ULS) and sectional properties of the four-lane,

six-box girder bridge at each section. It also shows the calculation of total factored tension at top and bottom flanges for composite sections at the positive and negative moment

4.1.5 Overall Bridge Design Efficiency Comparison for Four-Lane Bridge

Table 4.25 shows the design efficiency summary of the four-lane, three-box girder bridge. It contains ULS after composite action for shear, ULS before composite action for positive moment, ULS after composite action for positive moment, ULS before composite action for negative moment, ULS after composite action for negative moment, SLS control of permanent deflection stress check, FLS bottom flange stress check, and control of vibrations, SLS at each span reference point. Table 26 shows the design efficiency summary of the fourlane, four-box girder bridge. Table 27 shows the design efficiency summary of the four-lane, five-box girder bridge, and Table 28 shows the design efficiency summary of the four-lane, six-box girder bridge. They all include same design controls, which are mentioned for Table 4.25.

4.2. Results Summary for Three-Lane Bridge Design
This section presents the results for four lane bridge configurations.

4.2.1 Three-Box Girder Bridge Design Results
Figure 4.11 shows the cross-section dimensions of the three-lane, three-box girder bridge and longitudinal and transverse reinforcements in the rectangular concrete block within the effective slab width. Figure 4.12 shows that the bridge is divided into 20 segments, lOin each span. This implies 21 span reference points starting from the left end to the right end of the bridge. It should be noted 11 is located at the pier. Figure 4.13 shows the total effective slab width for one box-girder for three-lane, three-box girder bridge.

Table 4.30 shows the thicknesses and lengths of three-box girder bridge elements, such as slab, top flange, webs, bottom flange at each segment of the three-box girder bridge. Table 4.31 shows the calculation of moment resistance before composite action. It presents factored moment, Mr , and moment resistance, before composite action, Mr , and the moment performance of the three-lane, three-box girder bridge. Table 4.32 presents resistance of composite sections for three-lane, three-box girder bridge. It checks the plastic neutral axis and calculates the depth of compressive stress block. Table 4.33 presents shear resistance for three-lane, three-box girder bridge. It presents the factored shear and factored moments at each section, it shows the calculation of Fer and Ft for specific web stiffener distances, shear resistance,V r, moment resistance, Mr, and combined shear and moment situation. Table 4.34 shows control of permanent deflections, SLS for three-lane, three-box girder bridge using Clause 10.11.4 of CHBDC (24) . It presents Md (SLS), Msd (SLS), ML (SLS), Mrd (ULS), Mrsd(ULS), MfL(ULS) and sectional properties of the three-lane, three-box girder bridge at

146

each section. It also shows the calculation of total factored tension at top and bottom flanges for composite sections at the positive and negative moment regions. Table 4.35 presents fatigue stress range resistance, SLS for three-lane, three-box girder bridge. It shows sectional modulus of the three-box girder bridge at each section for composite and non-composite cases. It also presents
Mfd

(FLS),

Mfsd

(FLS),and

MiL

(FLS). It shows the calculation of

fatigue stress range resistance as well.

4.2.2 Four- Box Girder Bridge Design Results Summary

Figure 4.14 shows the cross-section dimensions of the three-lane, four-box girder bridge and longitudinal and transverse reinforcements in the rectangular concrete block within the effective slab width. Figure 4.15 shows that the bridge is divided into 20 segments, 10 in each span. This implies 21 span reference points starting from the left end to the right end of the bridge. It should be noted 11 is located at the pier. Figure 4.16 shows the total effective slab width for one box-girder for three-lane, four-box girder bridge.

Table 4.36 shows the thicknesses and lengths of four-box girder bridge elements, such as slab, top flange, webs, bottom flange at each segment of the three-box girder bridge. Table 4.37 shows the calculation of moment resistance before composite action. It presents factored moment, Mr , and moment resistance, before composite action, Mr , and the moment performance of the three-lane, four-box girder bridge. Table 4.38 presents resistance of composite sections for three-lane, four-box girder bridge. It checks the plastic neutral axis and calculates the depth of compressive stress block. Table 4.39 presents shear resistance for three-lane, four-box girder bridge. It presents the factored shear and factored moments at each section, it shows the calculation of Fer and Ft for specific web stiffener distances, shear resistance,V r, moment resistance, Mr, and combined shear and moment situation. Table 4.40 shows control of permanent deflections, SLS for three-lane, four-box girder bridge using Clause 10.11.4 of CHBDC (24) . It presents Md (SLS), Msd (SLS), ML (SLS), Mfd (ULS), Mfsd(ULS), MfL(ULS) and sectional properties of the four-lane, three-box girder bridge at each section. It also shows the calculation of total factored tension at top and bottom flanges for composite sections at the positive and negative moment regions. Table 4.41 presents

158

4.2.3 Five-Box Girder Bridge Design Results Summary Figure 4.17 shows the cross-section dimensions of the three-lane, five-box girder bridge and longitudinal and transverse reinforcements in the rectangular concrete block within the effective slab width. Figure 4.18 shows that the bridge is divided into 20 segments, lOin each span. This implies 21 span reference points starting from the left end to the right end of the bridge. It should be noted 11 is located at the pier. Figure 4.19 shows the total effective slab width for one box-girder for three-lane, five-box girder bridge.

Table 4.42 shows the thicknesses and lengths of five-box girder bridge elements, such as slab, top flange, webs, bottom flange at each segment of the five-box girder bridge. Table 4.43 shows the calculation of moment resistance before composite action. It presents factored moment, Mc , and moment resistance, before composite action, Mr , and the moment performance of the three-lane, five-box girder bridge. Table 4.44 presents resistance of composite sections for three-lane, five-box girder bridge for positive moment regions. It checks the plastic neutral axis and calculates the depth of compressive stress block. Table 4.45 presents shear resistance for three-lane, five-box girder bridge. It presents the factored shear and factored moments at each section, it shows the calculation ofFer and Ft for specific web stiffener distances, shear resistance,V r, moment resistance, Mr, and combined shear and moment situation. Table 4.46 shows control of permanent deflections, SLS for three-lane, five-box girder bridge using Clause 10.11.4 of CHBDC (24) . It presents Md (SLS), Msd (SLS), ML (SLS), Mfd (ULS), MCsd(ULS), MfL(ULS) and sectional properties of the threelane, five-box girder bridge at each section. It also shows the calculation of total factored tension at top and bottom flanges for composite sections at the positive and negative

It shows sectional modulus of the five-box girder bridge at each section for composite and non-composite cases. It also presents Mfd (FLS), shows the calculation of fatigue stress range resistance as well.

4.2.4 Overall Bridge Design Efficiency Comparison for Three-Lane Bridge

Table 4.48 shows the design efficiency summary ofthe three-lane, three-box girder bridge. It contains ULS after composite action for shear, ULS before composite action for positive moment, ULS after composite action for positive moment, ULS before composite action for negative moment, ULS after composite action for negative moment, SLS control of permanent deflection stress check, FLS bottom flange stress check, and control of vibrations, SLS at each span reference point. Table 4.49 shows the design efficiency summary of the three-lane, four-box girder bridge. Table 4.50 shows the design efficiency summary of the three-lane, five-box girder bridge. They all include same design controls, which are mentioned for Table 4.48

CHAPTER V

CONCLUSION
Based on the results obtained in this study, it is concluded that the three-box girder bridge is the most cost effective bridge for four-lane bridge case. On the other hand, for three-lane bridge case, three-box girder bridge is the most cost effective one as well.

For four-lane bridge case, as the number of girders are increased, the stress ranges for fatigue increases. For six-box girder bridges, it gets over 41.5 MPa. Vertical Shear

distribution factor Fv is greater than the moment distribution factor Fm for four-box, fivebox, and six-box girder bridge. Six-box girder bridge does not satisfy the criteria of fatigue stress range.

The three-box girder bridge design results in a 0.8% reduction in girder steel content when compared to four-box girder bridge, 12.2% reduction in girder steel content when compared five-box girder bridge, and 25.5% reduction in steel girder content when compared to six-box girder bridge.

For three-lane bridge case, as the number of girders are increased, the stress ranges for fatigue increases as happened in the four lane bridge case. The three-box girder bridge design is resulted in a 9.7% reduction in girder steel content when compared to four-box girder bridge, and 21.4% reduction in girder steel content when compared five-box girder bridge.

VITA AUCTORIS
I was born in 1967 in Istanbul city in Turkey. I had been in various cities In Turkey, such as Erzurum, Erzincan, Rize,Trabzon and Istanbul, from primary school to University because of my father's major, a Medical Doctor.

I completed my Bachelor Engineering degree in Civil Engineering in Istanbul Technical University, Istanbul, in 1989. Then I decided to start my Master of Applied Science

program at the same university. I graduated as a MASc Civil Engineer in July 1992.

After graduating from Istanbul Technical University in 1992, I had worked various construction and consulting companies mainly in Istanbul and surrounding areas, sometimes as a field engineer on the site or a design engineer in the office. I had worked on both steel and concrete structures as a field engineer and design engineer until December 2000.

In 2001, my Canadian life started. I started my Master of Engineering program in Ryerson University in September 2001. I learnt Canadian way of Engineering. I learnt the Canadian Steel Structure Code, and the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code.

APPENDIX A:
FOUR-LANE, FOUR-BOX GIRDER BRIDGE DESIGN RESULTS

A.t Design Procedure for Four-Lane, Four-Box Girder Bridge A.1.t Design Code: Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code of 2000, CAN/CSA-S6-00. A.t.2 Roadway: Class A, 4-lane undivided urban roadway. A.1.3 Design Approach: Longitudinal moments and shear forces are calculated using the "simplified method of analysis" for both dead and live loads, in accordance with clauses 5.6.1 and 5.7.1 of the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (24). The "empirical method of analysis" will be used for the deck slab in accordance with clauses 8.18.4 and 8.15.5 of Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (24). See, four-lane, four-box girder, Figure 4.1 for Cross-Section and Figure 4.2 for Girder Elevation.

A.2 Material Properties A.2.t Steel: G40.21 grade 350A and 350AT (Atmospheric resistant, notch-tough). Modulus of elasticity: Es = 200,000 MPa, Shear Modulus: Gs = 77,000 MPa. Minimum specified yield stress: Fy = 350 MPa (structural steel), fy = 400 MPa. (reinforcing bars). The main density: 7800 kg I m 3 ; material density: 77 kN 1m3 A.2.2 Concrete: 28-day strength:
·

fc' = 30 Mpa

Mass density: y = 2400 kg I m 3 ; Material density: 23.5 kN 1m3 · Modulus of elasticity, Clause 8.4.1.7 ofCHBDC (24): Ee = 24800 Mpa.

A-I

A.2.3 Design Lanes: Deck width, We = 15 m. Number of design lanes, n=4. Design lane width: We = We / n = 15/4 = 3.75 m, clause 3.8.2 ofCHBDC (24). Modification factor for multi-lane loading: n=4, RL = 0.7, clause 3.8.4.2 of Canadian Highway and Bridge Design Code (24). A.2.4 Dynamic Load Allowance: Truck load, 3 axles or more (ULS, SLS and FLS): DLA = 0.25 (Clause 3.8.4.5 of Canadian Highway and Bridge Design Code (24)). A.2.S Vibrations and Serviceability: The bridge is designed for occasional pedestrian use.

A.3 Cross Section Design: For for four-lane, four-box girder bridge section: A 1600 mm web depth was selected for the bridge, resulting in a span-to-depth ratio of 30. The roadway deck features a 225 mm concrete slab topped by a 90 mm asphalt wearing surface. A.3.t Requirements of the "Simplified Method of Analysis" for Live Load Clause 5.7.1.1 ofthe CHBDC (24) specifies few limitations for the use of simplified design method of analysis as follows: a) The bridge width is constant. b) Support conditions corresponds to line supports. c) Skew limits are satisfied (the bridge is unskewed).

A.3.2 Requirements of the "Empirical Design Method" of Deck Slabs Clause 8.18.4.1 of the CHBDC (24) specifies limitations for the use of the simplified design method for deck slabs as follows: a) The deck slab is composite with the supporting, parallel girders. b) Maximum girder web spacing: 2490 mm < 18 x Slab thickness = 18 x 225 = 4050 mm. c) The maximum girder web spacing: 2490 mm < 4000 mm. d) The requirements of section 10 ofCHBDC (24) regarding longitudinal reinforcement apply. e) The "empirical design method" requires a maximum intermediate cross frame spacing of8 m as stated in clause 8.18.5 ofCHBDC (24).

A.7 Section Properties Four different sets of section properties are calculated, depending on the construction stage and type of loading: 1. 2. 3. Before composite action bare steel girder only. After composite action, 1 x n: live loads (Sn). After composite action, 3 x n : dead loads applied after composite action, to account for creep and shrinkage an accordance with Clause 10.11.3 (S3n)' 4. After composite action, negative moment regions: steel girder and the reinforcing bars only (S').

A.7.1 Steel- Concrete Modular Ratio
Short-term loads: n = Es I Ee = 200,000 I 24,800 = 8.06 Long-term loads (including creep and shrinkage):

A-12

3n = 3 x 8.06 = 24.2

A.7.2 Stiffness Analysis

Negative bending moments at the supports are computed assuming full structural continuity, that is, using uncracked composite sections. Therefore, the analysis is based on fully composite sections using a modular ratio of 1 x n in both positive and negative moment regIOns.

A.9 Simplified Method of Analysis - Live Load Amplification Factors
Live load amplification factors (Fm and Fv) enable the calculation of longitudinal moments and shear forces using a simple, one-dimensional, line element mode, provided the requirements the "Simplified Method of Analysis" given in clause 5.7.1 of CHBDC (24) are satisfied.

A.I0.4 Shear Resistance

The shear resistance is calculated in accordance with clauses 10.10.5.1 and 10.12.3 of CHBDC (24). In the following case, the shear resistance of the trapezoidal box girder, Vr, is taken equal to the sum of the resistances of each web, multiplied by cose , where e is the angle of inclination of the web plate with respect to vertical.

The stiffened box girder has been divided longitudinally into different zones, in addition to the anchor panel zone located at the abutments, to determine shear resistance. Each zone has a different web stiffener spacing. The resisting shear stress of a stiffened web panel consists of two components, Fs = Fer + Ft, where Fer is the shear buckling stress, and Ft is the tension field component.

Web inclination angle: e = 14.0° , cose = 0.970 Factored shear force applied on the girder: Vf= 666.67 kN (ULS after composite action) Web height, h = 1600 / cose = 1600 / 0.97 = 1649 mm Web slenderness ratio, h/w = 1649 / 10 = 164.9 3150 / --.JFy = 168 > 164.9 Longitudinal stiffeners are not required (clause 10.10.4.2). Web area: Aw = 2hw = 2 x 1649 x 10 = 32,980 mm2

A-39

Try unstiffened web , a I h = 00 Shear buckling coefficient: kv = 5.34 Fer= 180,000 kv I (h/wi = 180,000 x 5.34 1164.92 = 35.35 Mpa Ft = 0 Shear stress: Fs = Fer = 35.35 MPa Girder Shear resistance : Vr =~s Aw Fs cosS = 0.95 x 32,980 x 35.35 x cos(14.0) = 1074.60 kN > 666.67 kN

A.13 Control of Cracking, SLS
The requirements for control of cracking of the concrete slab are given in clause 8.12 of CHBDC (24). However, since the slab reinforcement is designed in accordance with the empirical design method, these requirements are waived, clause 8.18.1 of CHBDC (24).

A.14 Superstructure Vibrations, SLS
The maximum deflection due to a single truck, including dynamic allowance, should not exceed the limit given in CHBDC (24), figure 3.4.4. The deflection limit depends on the fundamental frequency of the bridge and the anticipated pedestrian use. Because the two spans are equal, a simplified method will be used to estimate the fundamental frequency.

A.IS Fatigue
Fatigue stress ranges are based on FLS moments computed using the live load of a single truck, clause 5.7.1.2.2 of CHBDC (24). Load induced fatigue may be disregarded at locations where the compressive stress due to permanent loads is at least twice the maximum tensile stress due to the live loads, clause 10.17.2.1 of CHBDC (24). The following design requirement must be satisfied.

0.52 fSf < FSf (clause 10.17.2.2 ofCHBDC (24))

where fSf is the calculated stress range due to the passage of a CL-W truck and F Sf is the fatigue stress range resistance of a member or a detail.

A.IS.I Detail Categories
Top flange: Category C (stud-type shear connectors), clause 10.17.2.4 ofCHBDC (24). APPENDIXB:
FOUR-LANE, FIVE-BOX GIRDER BRIDGE DESIGN RESULTS

B.t Design Procedure, for Four-Lane, Five-Box Girder Bridge B.t.t Design Code:

Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code of 2000, CAN/CSA-S6-00.
B.1.2 Roadway:

Class A, 4-lane undivided urban roadway
B.1.3 Design Approach:

Longitudinal moments and shear forces are calculated using the "simplified method of analysis" for both dead and live loads, in accordance with clauses 5.6.1 and 5.7.1 of the Canadian Highway and Bridge Design Code (24). The "empirical method of analysis" will be used for the deck slab in accordance with clauses 8.18.4 and 8.15.5 of the Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (24). See four-lane, five-box girder, Figure 4.4 for cross-section and Figure 4.5 for Girder Elevation.

B.2 Material Properties B.2.t Steel:

G40.21 grade 350A and 350AT (Atmospheric resistant, notch-tough) Modulus of elasticity: Es = 200,000 Mpa, Shear Modulus: Gs = 77,000 Mpa Minimum specified yield stress: Fy = 350 Mpa (structural steel) fy = 400 Mpa (reinforcing bars). The main density: 7800 kg I m 3; material density: 77 kN 1m3
B.2.2 Concrete:

28-day strength:

fc' = 30 Mpa

Mass density: y = 2400 kg I m 3 Modulus of elasticity, Clause 8.4.1.7 of CHBDC (24): Ec = 24 800 Mpa.

B-1

B.2.3 Design Lanes:

Deck width, We = 15 m Number of design lanes, n=4 Design lane width: We = We / n = 15/4 = 3.75 m, clause 3.8.2 ofCHBDC (24). Modification factor for multi-lane loading: n=4, RL = 0.7, clause 3.8.4.2 of Canadian Highway and Bridge Design Code (24).
B.2.4 Dynamic Load Allowance:

Truck load, 3 axles or more (ULS, SLS and FLS): DLA = 0.25, clause 3.8.4.5 of Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (24).
B.2.S Vibrations and Serviceability:

The bridge is designed for occasional pedestrian use.

B.3 Cross Section Design:

For four-lane, five-box girder bridge section A 1600 mm web depth was selected for the bridge, resulting in a span-to-depth ratio of 30. The roadway deck features a 225 mm concrete slab topped by a 90 mm asphalt wearing surface.
B.3.l Requirements of the "Simplified Method of Analysis" for Live Load

Clause 5.7.1.1 of the CHBDC (24) specifies few limitations for. the use of simplified design method of analysis as follows: a) The bridge width is constant.
b) Support conditions corresponds to line supports.

c) Skew limits are satisfied (the bridge is unskewed).

B-2

d) Horizontal curvature limits are satisfied (The bridge is straight). e) The length ofthe slab taper near free edges does not exceed 2.5 m.

f) There are at least 3 equally spaced longitudinal girders of equal flexural rigidity.
g) Slab overhang: 1000 mm < 60% of spacing between two outermost webs = 0.60 x 1980 = 1188 mm 1000 mm < 1800 mm. h) The provisions A5.1(a) ofCHBDC(24) apply. i) Each spine (or box) has only 2 webs, and the conditions of clause 10.12.5.1 of CHBDC (24) apply.

B.3.2 Requirements of the "Empirical Design Method" of Deck Slabs
Clause 8.18.4.1 of the CHBDC (24) specifies limitations for the use of the simplified design method for deck slabs as follows: a) The deck slab is composite with the supporting, parallel girders. b) Maximum girder web spacing: 2000 mm < 18 x Slab thickness = 18 x 225 c) The maximum girder web spacing: 2000 mm < 4000 mm. d) The requirements of section 10 ofCHBDC (24) regarding longitudinal reinforcement apply. e) The "empirical design method" requires a maximum intermediate cross frame spacing of8 m as stated in clause 8.18.5 ofCHBDC (24).

B.7 Section Properties
Four different sets of section properties are calculated, depending on the construction stage and type of loading: 1. 2. 3. Before composite action bare steel girder only. After composite action, 1 x n: live loads (Sn ). After composite action, 3 x n : dead loads applied after composite action, to account for creep and shrinkage an accordance with Clause 10.11.3 (S3n). 4. After composite action, negative moment regions: steel girder and the reinforcing bars only (S ').

B.7.1 Steel- Concrete Modular Ratio
Short-term loads: n = Es I Ee = 200,000 I 24,800 = 8.06 Long-term loads (including creep and shrinkage):

B-12

3n = 3 x 8.06 = 24.2

B.7.2 Stiffness Analysis

Negative bending moments at the supports are computed assuming full structural continuity, that is, using uncracked composite sections. Therefore, the analysis is based on fully composite sections using a modular ratio of I x n in both positive and negative moment regions.

B.10 Resistance of Composite Sections-Positive Moment
In positive moment regions, the composite moment resistance is calculated using a fully plastic stress distribution, clauses 10.11.6.2 and 10.12.5.1 of CHBDC (24), with the portion of the web in compression limited to a depth of 850 w / ~Fy . Where section properties change at a reference point, the least properties are assumed.

B.I0.4 Shear Resistance

The shear resistance is calculated in accordance with clauses 10.10.5.1 and 10.12.3 of CHBDC (24). In the following example, the shear resistance of the trapezoidal box girder, Vr, is taken equal to the sum of the resistances of each web, multiplied by cosS , where S is the angle of inclination of the web plate with respect to vertical.

The stiffened box girder has been divided longitudinally into different zones, in addition to the anchor panel zone located at the abutments, to determine shear resistance. Each zone has a different web stiffener spacing. The resisting shear stress of a stiffened web panel consists of two components, Fs = Fer + Ft, where Fer is the shear buckling stress, and Ft is the tension field component.

Web inclination angle: S = 14.0° , cosS = 0.970 Factored shear force applied on the girder: Vf= 548 kN (ULS after composite action) Web height, h = 1600 I cosS = 1600 I 0.97 = 1649 mm Web slenderness ratio, hlw = 1649 I 10 = 164.9
3150 I--iFy = 168> 164.9 Longitudinal stiffeners are not required (clause 10.10.4.2).

Web area: Aw = 2hw = 2 x 1649 x 10 = 32,980 mm2

B.12 Shear Resistance of Anchor Panels B.12.1 Anchor Panel Zone, Near Abutments
It is usually impractical to provide effective means of anchoring the tension field in an end

panel. Therefore, the end panels are proportioned to serve as anchor panels such that tension fields can be developed in the interior panels. The shear resistance of end panels is calculated by setting Ft = 0, clause 10.10.5.1 ofCHBDC (24).

B.13 Control of Cracking, SLS

The requirements for control of cracking of the concrete slab are given in clause 8.12 of CHBDC (24). However, since the slab reinforcement is designed in accordance with the empirical design method, these requirements are waived, clause 8.18.1 of CHBDC 24).

B.14 Superstructure vibrations, SLS

The maximum deflection due to a single truck, including dynamic allowance, should not exceed the limit given in CHBDC (24), Figure 3.4.4. The deflection limit depends on the fundamental frequency of the bridge and the anticipated pedestrian use. Because the two spans are equal, a simplified method will be used to estimate the fundamental frequency.

B.15 Fatigue

Fatigue stress ranges are based on FLS moments computed using the live load of a single truck Clause 5.7.1.2.2 of CHBDC (24). Load induced fatigue may be disregarded at locations . where the compressive stress due to permanent loads is at least twice the maximum tensile stress due to the live loads, clause 10.17.2.1 of CHBDC (24). The following design requirement must be satisfied.

0.52 fSf < FSf (Clause 10.17.2.2 of CHBDC (24)).

where fSf is the calculated stress range due to the passage of a CL-W truck and FSf is the fatigue stress range resistance of a member or a detail.

APPENDIXC:
FOUR LANE SIX-BOX GIRDER BRIDGE DESIGN RESULTS

C.I Design Procedure for Four-Lane, Six-Box Girder Bridge C.l.I Design Code:

Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code of 2000, CAN/CSA-S6-00.
C.l.2 Roadway:

Class A, 4-lane undivided urban roadway.
C.l.3 Design Approach:

Longitudinal moments and shear forces are calculated using the "simplified method of analysis" for both dead and live loads, in accordance with clauses 5.6.1 and 5.7.1 of the Canadian Highway and Bridge Design Code. The "empirical method of analysis" will be used for the deck slab in accordance with clauses 8.18.4 and 8.15.5 ofthe Canadian Highway Bridge Design Code (24) . See four-lane, six-box girder, Figure 4.7 for Cross-Section and Figure 4.8 for Girder Elevation.

C.2 Material Properties C.2.1 Steel:

G40.21 grade 350A and 350AT (Atmospheric resistant, notch-tough) Modulus of elasticity: Es = 200,000 Mpa, Shear Modulus: Gs = 77,000 Mpa Minimum specified yield stress: Fy = 350 Mpa (structural steel) fy = 400 Mpa (reinforcing bars). The main density: 7800 kg I m 3; material density: 77 kN 1m3
C.2.2 Concrete:

28-day strength:

fc' = 30 Mpa

Mass density: y = 2400 kg I m 3 Modulus of elasticity, clause 8.4.1.7 ofCHBDC (24): Ee = 24800 Mpa.

C-I

C.2.3 Design Lanes:

Deck width, We = 15 m Number of design lanes, n=4 Design lane width: We = We / n = 15 /4 = 3.75 m, clause 3.8.2 ofCHBDC (24). Modification factor for multi-lane loading: n=4, RL = 0.7, clause 3.8.4.2 of Canadian Highway and Bridge Design Code (24).
C.2.4 Dynamic Load Allowance:

Truck load, 3 axles or more (ULS, SLS and FLS): DLA = 0.25, clause 3.8.4.5 of Canadian Highway and Bridge Design Code (24).
C.2.S Vibrations and Serviceability:

The bridge is designed for occasional pedestrian use.

C.3 Cross Section Design:

For four-lane, six-box girder bridge section: A 1600 mm web depth was selected for the bridge, resulting in a span-to-depth ratio of 30. The roadway deck features a 225 mm concrete slab topped by a 90 mm asphalt wearing surface.
C.3.1 Requirements of the "Simplified Method of Analysis" for Live Load

Clause 5.7.1.1 of the CHBDC (24) specifies few limitations for the use of simplified design method of analysis as follows: a) The bridge width is constant.
b) Support conditions corresponds to line supports.

c) Skew limits are satisfied (the bridge is unskewed).

C-2

d) Horizontal curvature limits are satisfied (The bridge is straight). e) The length of the slab taper near free edges does not exceed 2.5 m.

f) There are at least 3 equally spaced longitudinal girders of equal flexural rigidity.
g) Slab overhang: 1000 mm < 60% of spacing between two outermost webs = 0.60 x 1980 = 1188 mm 1000 mm < 1800 mm h) The provisions A5.1(a) ofCHBDC (24) apply. i) Each spine (or box) has only 2 webs, and the conditions of clause 10.12.5.1 of CHBDC (24) apply.

C.3.2 Requirements of the "Empirical Design Method" of Deck Slabs
Clause 8.18.4.1 of the CHBDC (24) specifies limitations for the use of the simplified design method for deck slabs as follows: a) The deck slab is composite with the supporting, parallel girders. b) Maximum girder web spacing: 1660 mm < 18 x Slab thickness = 18 x 225 c) The maximum girder web spacing: 1660 mm < 4000 mm. d) The requirements of section 10 ofCHBDC (24) regardinglongitudinal reinforcement apply. e) The "empirical design method" requires a maximum intermediate cross frame spacing of8 m as stated in clause 8.18.5 ofCHBDC (24).

Total longitudinal reinforcement ratio (top and bottom) : p=0.0147> 1% (Clause 10.11.53.2) % Top Bars = 0.0105 / 0.0147 = 0.714 > 2/3

C.6.5 Transverse Slab Moments in the Cantilever Overhang
Since the empirical design method is used for the transverse reinforcement, only the transverse moments due to loads on the overhang need be checked, clause 8.18.1 of CHBDC (24). Moments due to wheel loads on sidewalk are calculated using, clauses 3.8.4.4 and 5.7.1.6.1 of CHBDC (24). Design assumptions for this case are based on the 225 mm structural slab only, conservatively ignoring the stiffness contributions of the sidewalk and barrier.

C.7 Section Properties

Four different sets of section properties are calculated, depending on the construction stage and type of loading: 1. 2. 3. Before composite action bare steel girder only After composite action, 1 x n: live loads (Sn ) After composite action, 3 x n : dead loads applied after composite action, to account for creep and shrinkage an accordance with Clause 10.11.3 (S3n) 4. After composite action, negative moment regions: steel girder and the reinforcing bars only (S')

C.7.1 Steel- Concrete Modular Ratio

Short-term loads: n = Es I Ee = 200,000 I 24,800 = 8.06 Long-term loads (including creep and shrinkage):

C-12

3n = 3 x 8.06 = 24.2

C.7.2 Stiffness Analysis

Negative bending moments at the supports are computed assuming full structural continuity, that is, using uncracked composite sections. Therefore, the analysis is based on fully composite sections using a modular ratio of 1 x n in both positive and negative moment regIOns.

C.IO Resistance of Composite Sections-Positive Moment In positive moment regions, the composite moment resistance is calculated using a fully plastic stress distribution, clauses 10.11.6.2 and 10.12.5.1 of CHBDC (24), with the portion of the web in compression limited to a depth of 850 w / -VFy . Where section properties change at a reference point, the least properties are assumed.

C.I0.4 Shear Resistance

The shear resistance is calculated in accordance with clauses 10.10.5.1 and 10.12.3 of CHBDC (24). In the following example, the shear resistance of the trapezoidal box girder, Vr, is taken equal to the sum of the resistances of each web, multiplied by cosS , where S is the angle of inclination of the web plate with respect to vertical.

The stiffened box girder has been divided longitudinally into different zones, in addition to the anchor panel zone located at the abutments, to determine shear resistance. Each zone has a different web stiffener spacing. The resisting shear stress of a stiffened web panel consists of two components, Fs = Fer + Fh where Fer is the shear buckling stress, and Ft is the tension field component.

Web inclination angle: S = 14.0° , cosS = 0.970 Factored shear force applied on the girder: Vf= 445 kN (ULS after composite action) Web height, h = 1600/ cosS = 1600/0.97 = 1649 mm Web slenderness ratio, h/w = 1649/10 = 164.9

3150/ ..JFy = 168> 164.9 Longitudinal stiffeners are not required (clause 10.10.4.2).
Web area: Aw = 2hw = 2 x 1649 x 10 = 32,980 mm2

C.13 Control of Cracking, SLS

The requirements for control of cracking of the concrete slab are given in clause 8.12 of CHBDC (24). However, since the slab reinforcement is designed in accordance with the empirical design method, these requirements are waived, clause 8.18.1 ofCHBDC (24).

C.14 Superstructure Vibrations, SLS

The maximum deflection due to a single truck, including dynamic allowance, should not exceed the limit given in CHBDC (24), figure 3.4.4. The deflection limit depends on the fundamental frequency of the bridge and the anticipated pedestrian use. Because the two spans are equal, a simplified method will be used to estimate the fundamental frequency.

C.1S Fatigue

Fatigue stress ranges are based on FLS moments computed using the live load of a single truck, clause 5.7.1.2.2 of CHBDC (24). Load induced fatigue may be disregarded at locations where the compressive stress due to permanent loads is at least twice the maximum tensile stress due to the live loads clause 10.17.2.1 of CHBDC (24). The following design requirement must be satisfied.

0.52 fsf < FSf (Clause 10.17.2.2 ofCHBDC (24))

where fSf is the calculated stress range due to the passage of a CL-W truck and FSf is the fatigue stress range resistance of a member or a detail.

C.1S.1 Detail Categories

Top flange: Category C (stud-type shear connectors), clause 10.17.2.4 ofCHBDC (24).
